     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the African country. For the ancient kingdom, see <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>. For the archipelago country in the Indian Ocean, see <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Mauretania_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Mauretania (disambiguation)">Mauretania (disambiguation)</a>.
      </div> 
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country in West Africa

      <p><b>Mauritania</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ËŒ/: secondary stress follows">ËŒ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/É’r/: 'or' in 'moral'">É’r</span><span title="/Éª/: 'i' in 'kit'">Éª</span><span title="/Ëˆ/: primary stress follows">Ëˆ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/eÉª/: 'a' in 'face'">eÉª</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/É™/: 'a' in 'about'">É™</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ËŒ/: secondary stress follows">ËŒ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/É”Ër/: 'ar' in 'war'">É”Ër</span><span title="/Éª/: 'i' in 'kit'">Éª</span></span>-/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Mauritania.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/En-us-Mauritania.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Mauritania.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-:0_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ù…ÙˆØ±ÙŠØªØ§Ù†ÙŠØ§</span>â€, <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">MÅ«rÄ«tÄnyÄ</i></i>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Mauritanie</i>; <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber</a>: <i>Agawej</i> or <i>Cengit</i>; <a href="/wiki/Pulaar_language" title="Pulaar language">Pulaar</a>: <span lang="ff" dir="rtl" title="Fula-language text">ğ¤ƒğ¤®ğ¤ªğ¤­ğ¤¼ğ¤¢ğ¤²ğ¤­</span>â€ <i>Moritani</i>; <a href="/wiki/Wolof_language" title="Wolof language">Wolof</a>: <i>GÃ nnaar</i>; <a href="/wiki/Soninke_language" title="Soninke language">Soninke</a>: <i>Murutaane</i>), officially the <b>Islamic Republic of Mauritania</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ù…Ù‡ÙˆØ±ÙŠØ© Ø§Ù„Ø¥Ø³Ù„Ø§Ù…ÙŠØ© Ø§Ù„Ù…ÙˆØ±ÙŠØªØ§Ù†ÙŠØ©</span>â€), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Northwest Africa</a>. It is the eleventh largest <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> and is bordered by the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mauritania%E2%80%93Western_Sahara_border" title="Mauritaniaâ€“Western Sahara border">the north</a> and northwest, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Algeria%E2%80%93Mauritania_border" title="Algeriaâ€“Mauritania border">the northeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mali%E2%80%93Mauritania_border" title="Maliâ€“Mauritania border">the east and southeast</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mauritania%E2%80%93Senegal_border" title="Mauritaniaâ€“Senegal border">the southwest</a>. </p>
      <p>The country derives its name from the ancient <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>, which existed from the 3rd century BC into the 7th century AD in the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and West <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>. Prior to the Islamization of the area by Arab conquests in the 8th century, Mauritania was inhabited by Berbers since the 3rd century. Mauritania became a <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Mauritania" title="Colonial Mauritania">French colony</a> during the European <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>. Approximately 90% of Mauritania's land is within the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>; consequently, the population is concentrated in the south, where precipitation is slightly higher. The capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Nouakchott" title="Nouakchott">Nouakchott</a>, located on the Atlantic coast, which is home to around one-third of the country's 4 million people. The country's official religion is Islam, with almost the entire population being Sunni Muslims. <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> is the official language, with <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> also widely used due to its colonial history. Mauritania's largest ethnic group is the <a href="/wiki/Beidane" title="Beidane">Bidhan</a>, or white moors who make up around half the population. The second-largest group is the <a href="/wiki/Haratin" title="Haratin">Haratin</a>, or black moors who make up approximately one-third of the population. The rest of the population consists primarily of various <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan peoples</a>. </p>
      <p>Despite an abundance of natural resources, Mauritania remains poor. The country's economy is based on agriculture and livestock, and major industries include mining (particularly iron ore), petroleum, and fishing. </p>
      <p>After becoming independent from France in 1960 from <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a>, Mauritania's independence has been characterized by recurrent coups and periods of authoritarian military rule, the most recent of which was in <a href="/wiki/2008_Mauritanian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2008 Mauritanian coup d'Ã©tat">2008</a> and led by General <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Ould_Abdel_Aziz" title="Mohamed Ould Abdel Aziz">Mohamed Ould Abdel Aziz</a>. On 16 April 2009, Aziz resigned from the military to run for president in the 19 July elections, which he won.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2019_Mauritanian_presidential_election" title="2019 Mauritanian presidential election">2019 Mauritanian presidential election</a> was reported as the country's first <a href="/wiki/Peaceful_transition_of_power" title="Peaceful transition of power">peaceful transition of power</a> since independence. </p>
      <p>Mauritania has been criticized for its poor <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Mauritania" title="Human rights in Mauritania">human rights record</a>, including for <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Mauritania" title="Slavery in Mauritania">Mauritania's continued practice of slavery</a>, a result of the historical caste system, despite abolishing it in 1981 as the last country in the world to do so and criminalizing it in 2007. There have also been allegations of systematic torture by Mauritanian law enforcement. </p>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li> 
       </ul> 
      </div> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Mauritania takes its name from the ancient <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> kingdom in the third century BC, and later Roman province of <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a> that continued to the 7th century AD. The two territories do not overlap, though: Historical Mauritania was considerably further north than modern Mauritania, along the entire Western half of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> coast of Africa. </p>
      <p>Ultimately, the name therefore derives from the <a href="/wiki/Mauri_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Mauri people">Mauri people</a>, the Greek and therefore Roman <a href="/wiki/Exonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Exonym">exonym</a> for the Berber peoples of the region. The Mauri also lent their name to the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>, and probably to the modern African country of <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, which acquired its name from <a href="/wiki/Marrakesh" title="Marrakesh">Marrakesh</a>, whose name may mean People of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kush</a>, the "murr" part sharing the same root as the name Mauri. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_politics">History and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mauritania" title="History of Mauritania">History of Mauritania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Mauritania" title="Politics of Mauritania">Politics of Mauritania</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The ancient tribes of Mauritania were <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niger-Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Niger-Congo">Niger-Congo</a> people.<sup id="cite_ref-Stokes_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stokes-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bafour" title="Bafour">Bafours</a> were primarily agricultural, and among the first Saharan people to abandon their historically nomadic lifestyle. With the gradual desiccation of the Sahara, they headed south<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Many of the Berber tribes claimed Yemeni (and sometimes other Arab) origins. There is little evidence to support such claims, but a 2000 <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> study of Yemeni people suggested there might be some ancient connection between the peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Other peoples also migrated south past the Sahara to West Africa. In 1076 the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid" class="mw-redirect" title="Almoravid">Almoravids</a> conquered the large area of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Char_Bouba_war" title="Char Bouba war">Char Bouba war</a> (1644â€“74) was the unsuccessful final effort of the peoples to repel the <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemeni</a> <a href="/wiki/Maqil" title="Maqil">Maqil</a> Arab invaders. The invaders were led by the <a href="/wiki/Beni_Hassan" class="mw-redirect" title="Beni Hassan">Beni Hassan</a> tribe. The descendants of the Beni Hassan warriors became the <a href="/wiki/Hassane" title="Hassane">upper stratum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moorish</a> society. <a href="/wiki/Hassaniya" class="mw-redirect" title="Hassaniya">Hassaniya</a>, a bedouin <a href="/wiki/Arabic_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic dialect">Arabic dialect</a> that derives its name from the Beni Hassan, became the dominant language among the largely <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> population.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies-15">[15]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Berbers retained a niche influence by producing the majority of the region's <a href="/wiki/Marabout" title="Marabout">marabouts</a>: those who preserve and teach Islamic tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies-15">[15]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_history">Colonial history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Colonial history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Mauritania" title="Colonial Mauritania">Colonial Mauritania</a>
      </div>

        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Fort_of_Arguin_1721.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> ruled <a href="/wiki/Arguin" title="Arguin">Arguin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>: <i lang="pt">Arguim</i>) from 1445, after <a href="/wiki/Prince_Henry_the_Navigator" title="Prince Henry the Navigator">Prince Henry the Navigator</a> set up a <i><a href="/wiki/Factory_(trading_post)" title="Factory (trading post)">feitoria</a></i>, until 1633.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Vingboons_-_Aldus_verthoon_hem_%27t_casteel_Argijn_uyt_der_zee_(1665).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Johannes_Vingboons_-_Aldus_verthoon_hem_%27t_casteel_Argijn_uyt_der_zee_%281665%29.jpg/220px-Johannes_Vingboons_-_Aldus_verthoon_hem_%27t_casteel_Argijn_uyt_der_zee_%281665%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Johannes_Vingboons_-_Aldus_verthoon_hem_%27t_casteel_Argijn_uyt_der_zee_%281665%29.jpg/330px-Johannes_Vingboons_-_Aldus_verthoon_hem_%27t_casteel_Argijn_uyt_der_zee_%281665%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Johannes_Vingboons_-_Aldus_verthoon_hem_%27t_casteel_Argijn_uyt_der_zee_%281665%29.jpg/440px-Johannes_Vingboons_-_Aldus_verthoon_hem_%27t_casteel_Argijn_uyt_der_zee_%281665%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="970"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Vingboons_-_Aldus_verthoon_hem_%27t_casteel_Argijn_uyt_der_zee_(1665).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>After the Portuguese, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch</a>, and then the <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French</a>, took control of <a href="/wiki/Arguin" title="Arguin">Arguin</a> until abandoning it in 1685.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> <a href="/wiki/Franco-Trarzan_War_of_1825" title="Franco-Trarzan War of 1825">laid claim to the territories</a> of present-day Mauritania from the <a href="/wiki/Senegal_River" title="Senegal River">Senegal River</a> area and northwards, starting in the late 19th century. In 1901, <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Coppolani" title="Xavier Coppolani">Xavier Coppolani</a> took charge of the imperial mission.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Through a combination of strategic alliances with <a href="/wiki/Zawaya" title="Zawaya">Zawaya</a> tribes, and military pressure on the Hassane warrior nomads, he managed to extend French rule over the Mauritanian <a href="/wiki/Emirate" title="Emirate">emirates</a>. <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Trarza" title="Emirate of Trarza">Trarza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brakna" class="mw-redirect" title="Brakna">Brakna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tagant_Region" title="Tagant Region">Tagant</a> were occupied by the French armies in 1903â€“04, but the northern emirate of <a href="/wiki/Adrar_Plateau" title="Adrar Plateau">Adrar</a> held out longer, aided by the anti-colonial rebellion (or <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a>) of shaykh <a href="/wiki/Maa_al-Aynayn" class="mw-redirect" title="Maa al-Aynayn">Maa al-Aynayn</a>, as well by insurgents from Tagant and the other regions. Adrar was finally defeated militarily in 1912, and incorporated into the territory of Mauritania, which had been drawn up and planned in 1904. Mauritania was part of <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a> from 1920, as a <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a> and, then, a <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colony</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>French rule brought legal prohibitions against slavery and an end to inter-clan warfare. During the colonial period, 90% of the population remained nomadic. Many sedentary peoples, whose ancestors had been expelled centuries earlier, began to trickle back into Mauritania. The previous capital of the country under the French rule, <a href="/wiki/Saint-Louis,_Senegal" title="Saint-Louis, Senegal">Saint-Louis</a>, was located in Senegal, so when the country gained independence in 1960, Nouakchott, at the time little more than a fortified village (<i>"ksar"</i>), was chosen as the site of the new capital of Mauritania.<sup id="cite_ref-Pazzanita2008_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pazzanita2008-18">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After gaining independence, larger numbers of indigenous <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_African" class="mw-redirect" title="Sub-Saharan African">Sub-Saharan African</a> peoples (<a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Haalpulaar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soninke_people" title="Soninke people">Soninke</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wolof_people" title="Wolof people">Wolof</a>) entered Mauritania, moving into the area north of the <a href="/wiki/Senegal_River" title="Senegal River">Senegal River</a>. Educated in French language and customs, many of these recent arrivals became clerks, soldiers, and administrators in the new state. This occurred as the French militarily suppressed the most intransigent Hassane tribes in the north. This changed the former balance of power, and new conflicts arose between the southern populations and Moors. Between these groups stood African origins, who is part of the Arab society, integrated into a low-caste social position.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Unsure what this sentence is trying to convey, but it doesn't discernibly reflect anything in the cited source. (April 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Please help to fix this. (April 2019)">incomprehensible</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Contemporary_slavery" class="mw-redirect" title="Contemporary slavery">Modern-day slavery</a> still exists in different forms in Mauritania.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> According to some estimates, thousands of Mauritanians <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Mauritania" title="Slavery in Mauritania">are still enslaved</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> A 2012 <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> report, "Slavery's Last Stronghold," by John D. Sutter, describes and documents the ongoing slave-owning cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> This social discrimination is applied chiefly against the "black Moors" (Haratin) in the northern part of the country, where tribal elites among "white Moors" (<i>Bidh'an</i>, <a href="/wiki/Hassaniya_Arabic" title="Hassaniya Arabic">Hassaniya-speaking Arabs and Arabized Berbers</a>) hold sway.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Slavery practices exist also within the sub-Saharan African ethnic groups of the south. </p>
      <p>The great <a href="/wiki/Sahel_drought" title="Sahel drought">Sahel droughts</a> of the early 1970s caused massive devastation in Mauritania, exacerbating problems of poverty and conflict. The Arabized dominant elites reacted to changing circumstances, and to <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab nationalist">Arab nationalist</a> calls from abroad, by increasing pressure to <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabize</a> many aspects of Mauritanian life, such as law and the education system. This was also a reaction to the consequences of the French domination under the colonial rule. Various models for maintaining the country's cultural diversity have been suggested, but none have been successfully implemented. </p>
      <p>This ethnic discord was evident during inter-communal violence that broke out in April 1989 (the "<a href="/wiki/Mauritania%E2%80%93Senegal_Border_War" title="Mauritaniaâ€“Senegal Border War">Mauritaniaâ€“Senegal Border War</a>"), but has since subsided. Mauritania expelled some 70,000 sub-Saharan African Mauritanians in the late 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Ethnic tensions and the sensitive issue of slavery â€“&nbsp;past and, in some areas, present&nbsp;â€“ are still powerful themes in the country's political debate. A significant number from all groups seek a more diverse, pluralistic society. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_with_Western_Sahara">Conflict with Western Sahara</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Conflict with Western Sahara">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Central_mosque_in_Nouakchott.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Central_mosque_in_Nouakchott.jpg/220px-Central_mosque_in_Nouakchott.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Central_mosque_in_Nouakchott.jpg/330px-Central_mosque_in_Nouakchott.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Central_mosque_in_Nouakchott.jpg/440px-Central_mosque_in_Nouakchott.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Central_mosque_in_Nouakchott.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Nouakchott" title="Nouakchott">Nouakchott</a> is the capital and the largest city of Mauritania. It is one of the largest cities in the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Status_of_Western_Sahara" class="mw-redirect" title="Status of Western Sahara">Status of Western Sahara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiris_al-Gharbiyya" title="Tiris al-Gharbiyya">Tiris al-Gharbiyya</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> has concluded that in spite of some evidence of both Morocco's and Mauritania's legal ties prior to Spanish colonization, neither set of ties was sufficient to affect the application of the UN General Assembly <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Granting_of_Independence_to_Colonial_Countries_and_Peoples" title="Declaration on the Granting of Independence to Colonial Countries and Peoples">Declaration on the Granting of Independence to Colonial Countries and Peoples</a> to <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Mauritania, along with <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, annexed the territory of <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a> in 1976, with Mauritania taking the lower one-third at the request of Spain, a former imperial power. After several military losses from the <a href="/wiki/Polisario" class="mw-redirect" title="Polisario">Polisario</a> â€“&nbsp;heavily armed and supported by Algeria, the <a href="/wiki/Regional_power" title="Regional power">regional power</a> and rival to Morocco&nbsp;â€“ Mauritania withdrew in 1979. Its claims were taken over by Morocco. </p>
      <p>Due to economic weakness, Mauritania has been a negligible player in the territorial dispute, with its official position being that it wishes for an expedient solution that is mutually agreeable to all parties. While most of Western Sahara has been occupied by Morocco, the UN still considers the Western Sahara a territory that needs to express its wishes with respect to statehood. A referendum, originally scheduled for 1992, is still supposed to be held at some point in the future, under UN auspices, to determine whether or not the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Sahrawis" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahrawis">Sahrawis</a> wish to be independent, as the <a href="/wiki/Sahrawi_Arab_Democratic_Republic" title="Sahrawi Arab Democratic Republic">Sahrawi Arab Democratic Republic</a>, or to be part of Morocco. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Ould_Daddah_era_.281960.E2.80.931978.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ould_Daddah_era_(1960â€“1978)">Ould Daddah era (1960â€“1978)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ould Daddah era (1960â€“1978)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Mauritania" title="Constitution of Mauritania">Constitution of Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritanian_People%27s_Party" title="Mauritanian People's Party">Mauritanian People's Party</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moktar_Ould_Daddah" title="Moktar Ould Daddah">Moktar Ould Daddah</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Mauritania became an independent nation in November 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1964 <a href="/wiki/President_of_Mauritania" class="mw-redirect" title="President of Mauritania">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Moktar_Ould_Daddah" title="Moktar Ould Daddah">Moktar Ould Daddah</a>, originally installed by the French, formalized Mauritania as a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party state</a> with a new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Mauritania" title="Constitution of Mauritania">constitution</a>, setting up an <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian</a> presidential regime. Daddah's own <a href="/wiki/Parti_du_Peuple_Mauritanien" class="mw-redirect" title="Parti du Peuple Mauritanien">Parti du Peuple Mauritanien</a> (PPM) became the ruling organization in a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party system</a>. The President justified this on the grounds that Mauritania was not ready for western-style <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party democracy</a>. Under this one-party constitution, Daddah was reelected in uncontested elections in 1976 and 1978. </p>
      <p>Daddah was ousted in a <a href="/wiki/Military_Committee_for_National_Recovery" title="Military Committee for National Recovery">bloodless coup</a> on 10 July 1978. He had brought the country to near-collapse through the disastrous <a href="/wiki/History_of_Western_Sahara" title="History of Western Sahara">war</a> to <a href="/wiki/Annexation" title="Annexation">annex</a> the <a href="/wiki/Tiris_al-Gharbiyya" title="Tiris al-Gharbiyya">southern part</a> of <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a>, framed as an attempt to create a "<a href="/wiki/Greater_Mauritania" title="Greater Mauritania">Greater Mauritania</a>". </p> 
      <h3><span id="CMRN_and_CMSN_military_governments_.281978.E2.80.931984.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="CMRN_and_CMSN_military_governments_(1978â€“1984)">CMRN and CMSN military governments (1978â€“1984)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: CMRN and CMSN military governments (1978â€“1984)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Chinguetti-Guide.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Chinguetti-Guide.JPG/220px-Chinguetti-Guide.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Chinguetti-Guide.JPG/330px-Chinguetti-Guide.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Chinguetti-Guide.JPG/440px-Chinguetti-Guide.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Chinguetti-Guide.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Chinguetti" title="Chinguetti">Chinguetti</a> was a center of Islamic scholarship in West Africa.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Col." class="mw-redirect" title="Col.">Col.</a> <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Ould_Salek" title="Mustafa Ould Salek">Mustafa Ould Salek</a>'s <a href="/wiki/CMRN" class="mw-redirect" title="CMRN">CMRN</a> <a href="/wiki/Military_junta" title="Military junta"><i>junta</i></a> proved incapable of either establishing a strong base of power or extracting the country from its destabilizing conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Sahrawi</a> resistance movement, the <a href="/wiki/Polisario_Front" title="Polisario Front">Polisario Front</a>. It quickly fell, to be replaced by another military government, the <a href="/wiki/Military_Committee_for_National_Salvation" title="Military Committee for National Salvation">CMSN</a>. </p>
      <p>The energetic Colonel <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Khouna_Ould_Haidallah" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohamed Khouna Ould Haidallah">Mohamed Khouna Ould Haidallah</a> soon emerged as its strongman. By giving up all claims to Western Sahara, he found peace with the Polisario and improved relations with its main backer, Algeria. But relations with Morocco, the other party to the conflict, and its European ally France deteriorated. Instability continued, and Haidallah's ambitious reform attempts foundered. His regime was plagued by attempted coups and intrigue within the military establishment. It became increasingly contested due to his harsh and uncompromising measures against opponents; many dissidents were jailed, and some executed. In 1981 slavery was formally abolished by law, making Mauritania the last country in the world to do so. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Ould_Taya.27s_rule_.281984.E2.80.932005.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ould_Taya's_rule_(1984â€“2005)">Ould Taya's rule (1984â€“2005)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ould Taya's rule (1984â€“2005)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In December 1984, Haidallah was deposed by Colonel <a href="/wiki/Maaouya_Ould_Sid%27Ahmed_Taya" title="Maaouya Ould Sid'Ahmed Taya">Maaouya Ould Sid'Ahmed Taya</a>, who, while retaining tight military control, relaxed the political climate. Ould Taya moderated Mauritania's previous pro-Algerian stance, and re-established ties with Morocco during the late 1980s. He deepened these ties during the late 1990s and early 2000s as part of Mauritania's drive to attract support from Western states and Western-aligned Arab states. Mauritania has not rescinded its recognition of Polisario's Western Saharan exile government, and remains on good terms with Algeria. Its position on the Western Sahara conflict is, since the 1980s, one of strict neutrality. </p>
      <p>Ordinance 83.127, enacted 5 June 1983, launched the process of nationalization of all land not clearly the property of a documented owner, thus abolishing the traditional system of land tenure. Potential nationalization was based on the concept of "dead land",<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> i.e., property which has not been developed or on which obvious development cannot be seen. A practical effect was government seizure of traditional communal grazing lands.<sup id="cite_ref-MCT_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCT-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>42, 60</span></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_Mauritania" title="List of political parties in Mauritania">Political parties</a>, illegal during the military period, were legalized again in 1991. By April 1992, as civilian rule returned, 16 major political parties had been recognized; 12 major political parties were active in 2004. The <i><a href="/wiki/Parti_R%C3%A9publicain_D%C3%A9mocratique_et_Social" class="mw-redirect" title="Parti RÃ©publicain DÃ©mocratique et Social">Parti RÃ©publicain DÃ©mocratique et Social</a></i> (PRDS), formerly led by President Maaouya Ould Sid'Ahmed Taya, dominated Mauritanian politics after the country's first multi-party elections in April 1992, following the approval by <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a> of the current constitution in July 1991. President Taya won elections in 1992 and 1997. Most opposition parties boycotted the first legislative election in 1992. For nearly a decade the parliament was dominated by the PRDS. The opposition participated in municipal elections in Januaryâ€“February 1994, and in subsequent <a href="/wiki/Senate" title="Senate">Senate</a> elections â€“ most recently in April 2004 â€“ and gained representation at the local level, as well as three seats in the Senate. </p>
      <p>This period was marked by extensive ethnic violence and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Mauritania" title="Human rights in Mauritania">human rights abuses</a>. Between 1990 and 1991, a campaign of particularly extreme violence took place against a background of <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabization</a>, interference with blacks' association rights, expropriation and expatriation.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nouakchott_0518.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Nouakchott_0518.JPG/220px-Nouakchott_0518.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Nouakchott_0518.JPG/330px-Nouakchott_0518.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Nouakchott_0518.JPG/440px-Nouakchott_0518.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="1232"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nouakchott_0518.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Nouakchott" title="Nouakchott">Nouakchott</a>. The population of Nouakchott has increased from 20,000 in 1969 to almost 1 million in 2013.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In October 1987, the government allegedly uncovered a tentative <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'Ã©tat">coup d'Ã©tat</a> by a group of black army officers, backed, according to the authorities, by <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Fifty-one officers were arrested and subjected to interrogation and torture.<sup id="cite_ref-sy_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sy-33">[33]</a></sup> Heightened ethnic tensions were the catalyst for the <a href="/wiki/Mauritania%E2%80%93Senegal_Border_War" title="Mauritaniaâ€“Senegal Border War">Mauritaniaâ€“Senegal Border War</a>, which started as a result of a conflict in Diawara between Moorish Mauritanian herders and Senegalese farmers over grazing rights.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> On 9 April 1989, Mauritanian guards killed two Senegalese.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following the incident, several riots erupted in <a href="/wiki/Bakel,_Senegal" title="Bakel, Senegal">Bakel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">Dakar</a> and other towns in Senegal, directed against the mainly Arabized Mauritanians who dominated the local retail business. The rioting, adding to already existing tensions, led to a campaign of terror against black Mauritanians,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> who are often seen as 'Senegalese' by Bidha'an, regardless of their nationality. As low scale conflict with Senegal continued into 1990/91, the Mauritanian government engaged in or encouraged acts of violence and seizures of property directed against the Halpularen ethnic group. The tension culminated in an international airlift agreed to by Senegal and Mauritania under international pressure to prevent further violence. The Mauritanian Government expelled tens of thousands of black Mauritanians. Most of these so-called 'Senegalese' had no ties to Senegal, and many have been repatriated from Senegal and Mali after 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-sy_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sy-33">[33]</a></sup> The exact number of expulsions is not known but the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR) estimates that, as of June 1991, 52,995 Mauritanian refugees were living in Senegal and at least 13,000 in Mali.<sup id="cite_ref-MCT_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCT-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>27</span></sup> </p>
      <p>From November 1990 to February 1991, between 200 and 600 (depending on the sources) Fula and Soninke soldiers and/or political prisoners were executed or tortured to death by Mauritanian government forces. They were among 3,000 to 5,000 blacks&nbsp;â€“ predominantly soldiers and civil servants&nbsp;â€“ arrested between October 1990 and mid-January 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Some Mauritanian exiles believe that the number was as high as 5,000 on the basis of alleged involvement in an attempt to overthrow the government.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr-39">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The government initiated a military investigation but never released the results.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr-39">[39]</a></sup> In order to guarantee immunity for those responsible and to block any attempts at accountability for past abuses, the Parliament declared an amnesty<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> in June 1993 covering all crimes committed by the armed forces, security forces as well as civilians, between April 1989 and April 1992. The government offered compensation to families of victims, which a few accepted in lieu of settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr-39">[39]</a></sup> Despite this amnesty, some Mauritanians have denounced the involvement of the government in the arrests and killings.<sup id="cite_ref-MCT_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCT-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>87</span></sup> </p>
      <p>In the late 1980s, Ould Taya had established close co-operation with <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, and pursued a strongly <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab nationalist">Arab nationalist</a> line. Mauritania grew increasingly isolated internationally, and tensions with Western countries grew dramatically after it took a pro-Iraqi position during the <a href="/wiki/1991_Gulf_War" class="mw-redirect" title="1991 Gulf War">1991 Gulf War</a>. During the mid-to late 1990s, Mauritania shifted its foreign policy to one of increased co-operation with the US and Europe. It was rewarded with diplomatic normalization and aid projects. On 28 October 1999, Mauritania joined Egypt, Palestine, and Jordan as the only members of the Arab League to officially recognize Israel. Ould Taya also started co-operating with the United States in anti-terrorism activities, a policy which was criticized by some human rights organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> (See also <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Mauritania" title="Foreign relations of Mauritania">Foreign relations of Mauritania</a>.) </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Road_from_Nouakchott_to_Mauritanian.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Road_from_Nouakchott_to_Mauritanian.jpg/220px-Road_from_Nouakchott_to_Mauritanian.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Road_from_Nouakchott_to_Mauritanian.jpg/330px-Road_from_Nouakchott_to_Mauritanian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Road_from_Nouakchott_to_Mauritanian.jpg/440px-Road_from_Nouakchott_to_Mauritanian.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3036" data-file-height="2004"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Road_from_Nouakchott_to_Mauritanian.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Road from Nouakchott to the Mauritanianâ€“Senegalese border
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A group of current and former Army officers launched a violent and unsuccessful coup attempt on 8 June 2003. The leaders of the attempted coup escaped from the country, but some of them were caught, later on. Mauritania's <a href="/wiki/Mauritanian_presidential_election,_2003" class="mw-redirect" title="Mauritanian presidential election, 2003">presidential election</a>, its third since adopting the democratic process in 1992, took place on 7 November 2003. Six candidates, including Mauritania's first female and first Haratine (descended from the original inhabitants of the Tassili n'Ajjer and Acacus Mountains during the Epipalaeolithic era<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>) candidates, represented a wide variety of political goals and backgrounds. Incumbent President <a href="/wiki/Maaouya_Ould_Sid%27Ahmed_Taya" title="Maaouya Ould Sid'Ahmed Taya">Maaouya Ould Sid'Ahmed Taya</a> won reelection with 67.0% of the popular vote, according to the official figures, with <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Khouna_Ould_Haidalla" title="Mohamed Khouna Ould Haidalla">Mohamed Khouna Ould Haidalla</a> finishing second. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="August_2005_military_coup">August 2005 military coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: August 2005 military coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>On 3 August 2005, a military coup led by Colonel <a href="/wiki/Ely_Ould_Mohamed_Vall" title="Ely Ould Mohamed Vall">Ely Ould Mohamed Vall</a> ended Maaouya Ould Sid'Ahmed Taya's twenty-one years of rule. Taking advantage of Taya's attendance at the funeral of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi</a> <a href="/wiki/King_Fahd" class="mw-redirect" title="King Fahd">King Fahd</a>, the military, including members of the presidential guard, seized control of key points in the capital <a href="/wiki/Nouakchott" title="Nouakchott">Nouakchott</a>. The coup proceeded without loss of life. Calling themselves the Military Council for Justice and Democracy, the officers released the following statement: </p> 
      <dl>
       <dd>
        "The national armed forces and security forces have unanimously decided to put a definitive end to the oppressive activities of the defunct authority, which our people have suffered from during the past years."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
       </dd>
      </dl> 
      <p>The Military Council later issued another statement naming Colonel Vall as president and director of the national police force, the <i>SÃ»retÃ© Nationale</i>. Vall, once regarded as a firm ally of the now-ousted president, had aided Taya in the coup that had originally brought him to power, and had later served as his security chief. Sixteen other officers were listed as members of the Council. </p>
      <p>Though cautiously watched by the international community, the coup came to be generally accepted, with the military <i>junta</i> organizing elections within a promised two-year timeline. In a <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a> on 26 June 2006, Mauritanians overwhelmingly (97%) approved a new constitution which limited the duration of a president's stay in office. The leader of the <i>junta</i>, Col. Vall, promised to abide by the referendum and relinquish power peacefully. Mauritania's establishment of relations with <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>&nbsp;â€“ it is one of only three Arab states to recognize Israel&nbsp;â€“ was maintained by the new regime, despite widespread criticism from the opposition. They considered that position as a legacy of the Taya regime's attempts to curry favor with the West. </p>
      <p>Parliamentary and municipal elections in Mauritania took place on 19 November and 3 December 2006. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2007_presidential_elections">2007 presidential elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 2007 presidential elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sidi_Mohamed_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sidi_Mohamed_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi.jpg/220px-Sidi_Mohamed_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sidi_Mohamed_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi.jpg/330px-Sidi_Mohamed_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sidi_Mohamed_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi.jpg/440px-Sidi_Mohamed_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="697" data-file-height="914"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sidi_Mohamed_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Sidi_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi" title="Sidi Ould Cheikh Abdallahi">Sidi Ould Cheikh Abdallahi</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Mauritania's first fully democratic presidential elections took place on 11 March 2007. The elections effected the final transfer from military to civilian rule following the military coup in 2005. This was the first time since Mauritania gained independence in 1960 that it elected a president in a multi-candidate election.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The elections were won in a second round of voting by <a href="/wiki/Sidi_Ould_Cheikh_Abdallahi" title="Sidi Ould Cheikh Abdallahi">Sidi Ould Cheikh Abdallahi</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Ould_Daddah" title="Ahmed Ould Daddah">Ahmed Ould Daddah</a> a close second. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2008_military_coup">2008 military coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2008 military coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/2008_Mauritanian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2008 Mauritanian coup d'Ã©tat">2008 Mauritanian coup d'Ã©tat</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On 6 August 2008, the head of the presidential guards took over the president's palace in Nouakchott, a day after 48 lawmakers from the ruling party resigned in protest of President Abdallahi's policies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2013)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> The army surrounded key government facilities, including the state television building, after the president fired senior officers, one of them the head of the presidential guards.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The President, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Yahya_Ould_Ahmed_Waghef" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahya Ould Ahmed Waghef">Yahya Ould Ahmed Waghef</a>, and Mohamed Ould R'zeizim, Minister of Internal Affairs, were arrested. </p>
      <p>The coup was coordinated by General <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Ould_Abdel_Aziz" title="Mohamed Ould Abdel Aziz">Mohamed Ould Abdel Aziz</a>, former chief of staff of the Mauritanian Army and head of the presidential guard, who had recently been fired. Mauritania's presidential spokesman, Abdoulaye Mamadouba, said the President, Prime Minister, and Interior Minister had been arrested by renegade Senior Mauritanian army officers and were being held under house arrest at the presidential palace in the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In the apparently successful and bloodless coup, Abdallahi's daughter, Amal Mint Cheikh Abdallahi, said: "The security agents of the BASEP (Presidential Security Battalion) came to our home and took away my father."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The coup plotters, all dismissed in a presidential decree shortly beforehand, included Abdel Aziz, General Muhammad Ould Al-Ghazwani, General Philippe Swikri, and Brigadier General (Aqid) Ahmad Ould Bakri.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_coup">After the coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: After the coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:197px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mauritania-aziz-in-his-home-city-Akjoujt-15mar09_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2a/Mauritania-aziz-in-his-home-city-Akjoujt-15mar09_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="195" height="210" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="195" data-file-height="210"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mauritania-aziz-in-his-home-city-Akjoujt-15mar09_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Mohamed Ould Abdel Aziz in his hometown, <a href="/wiki/Akjoujt" title="Akjoujt">Akjoujt</a>, on 15 March 2009
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nouakchott-Dispersion_des_manifestants-2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nouakchott-Dispersion_des_manifestants-2011.jpg/220px-Nouakchott-Dispersion_des_manifestants-2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nouakchott-Dispersion_des_manifestants-2011.jpg/330px-Nouakchott-Dispersion_des_manifestants-2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Nouakchott-Dispersion_des_manifestants-2011.jpg/440px-Nouakchott-Dispersion_des_manifestants-2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nouakchott-Dispersion_des_manifestants-2011.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_Mauritanian_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011â€“12 Mauritanian protests">2011â€“12 Mauritanian protests</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A Mauritanian lawmaker, Mohammed Al Mukhtar, claimed that many of the country's people supported the takeover of a government that had become "an authoritarian regime" under a president who had "marginalized the majority in parliament."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The coup was also backed by Abdallahi's rival in the 2007 election, Ahmed Ould Daddah. However, Abdel Aziz's regime was isolated internationally, and became subject to diplomatic sanctions and the cancellation of some aid projects. It found few foreign supporters (among them Morocco, Libya and Iran), while Algeria, the United States, France and other European countries criticized the coup, and continued to refer to Abdallahi as the legitimate president of Mauritania. Domestically, a group of parties coalesced around Abdallahi to continue protesting the coup, which caused the junta to ban demonstrations and crack down on opposition activists. International and internal pressure eventually forced the release of Abdallahi, who was instead placed under house arrest in his home village. The new government broke off relations with Israel. In March 2010, Mauritania's female foreign minister Mint Hamdi Ould Mouknass announced that Mauritania had cut ties with Israel in a "complete and definitive way."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the coup, Abdel Aziz insisted on holding new presidential elections to replace Abdallahi, but was forced to reschedule them due to internal and international opposition. During the spring of 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Military_junta" title="Military junta">junta</a> negotiated an understanding with some opposition figures and international parties. As a result, Abdallahi formally resigned under protest, as it became clear that some opposition forces had defected from him and most international players, notably including France and Algeria, now aligned with Abdel Aziz. The United States continued to criticize the coup, but did not actively oppose the elections. </p>
      <p>Abdallahi's resignation allowed the <a href="/wiki/Mauritanian_presidential_election,_2009" class="mw-redirect" title="Mauritanian presidential election, 2009">election</a> of Abdel Aziz as civilian president, on 18 July, by a 52% majority. Many of Abdallahi's former supporters criticized this as a political ploy and refused to recognize the results. They argued that the election had been falsified due to junta control, and complained that the international community had let down the opposition. Despite complaints, the elections were almost unanimously accepted by Western, Arab and African countries, which lifted sanctions and resumed relations with Mauritania. By late summer, Abdel Aziz appeared to have secured his position and to have gained widespread international and internal support. Some figures, such as Senate chairman <a href="/wiki/Messaoud_Ould_Boulkheir" title="Messaoud Ould Boulkheir">Messaoud Ould Boulkheir</a>, continued to refuse the new order and call for Abdel Aziz's resignation. </p>
      <p>In February 2011, the waves of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> <a href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_Mauritanian_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011â€“12 Mauritanian protests">spread to Mauritania</a>, where thousands of people took to the streets of the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In November 2014, Mauritania was invited as a non-member guest nation to the G20 summit in Brisbane. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Mauritania" title="Demographics of Mauritania">Demographics of Mauritania</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Adrar-Mother%26daughter.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Adrar-Mother%26daughter.JPG/220px-Adrar-Mother%26daughter.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Adrar-Mother%26daughter.JPG/330px-Adrar-Mother%26daughter.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Adrar-Mother%26daughter.JPG/440px-Adrar-Mother%26daughter.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">

        </div>
       </div>
      </div>

      <p>As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Mauritania has a population of approximately 4.3 million. The local population is composed of three main ethnicities: <a href="/wiki/Sahrawi_people" title="Sahrawi people">Bidhan</a> or white Moors, <a href="/wiki/Haratin" title="Haratin">Haratin</a> or black moors, and <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africans</a>. 53% Bidhan, 30% Haratin, and 17% others. Local statistics bureau estimations indicate that the Bidhan represent around 53% of citizens. They speak <a href="/wiki/Hassaniya_Arabic" title="Hassaniya Arabic">Hassaniya Arabic</a> and are primarily of <a href="/wiki/Arab-Berber" title="Arab-Berber">Arab-Berber</a> origin. The Haratin constitute roughly 34% of the population. They are descendants of the original inhabitants of the Tassili n'Ajjer and Acacus Mountain sites during the <a href="/wiki/Epipalaeolithic" title="Epipalaeolithic">Epipalaeolithic</a> era. <sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>The remaining 13% of the population largely consists of various ethnic groups of West African descent. Among these are the <a href="/wiki/Niger-Congo_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Niger-Congo languages">Niger-Congo</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Halpulaar</a> (Fulbe), <a href="/wiki/Soninke_people" title="Soninke people">Soninke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bambara_people" title="Bambara people">Bambara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolof_people" title="Wolof people">Wolof</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;"> 
       <div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;"> 
        <table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;"> 
         <tbody>
          <tr style="background:#ddd">
           <th style="text-align:center;" colspan="5">Mauritania Religions<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup></th>
          </tr> 
          <tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;">

       </div> 
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Mauritania" title="Religion in Mauritania">Religion in Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Mauritania" title="Islam in Mauritania">Islam in Mauritania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Status_of_religious_freedom_in_Mauritania" class="mw-redirect" title="Status of religious freedom in Mauritania">Status of religious freedom in Mauritania</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nouakchott_camel_market2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nouakchott_camel_market2.jpg/220px-Nouakchott_camel_market2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nouakchott_camel_market2.jpg/330px-Nouakchott_camel_market2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nouakchott_camel_market2.jpg/440px-Nouakchott_camel_market2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3504" data-file-height="2336"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nouakchott_camel_market2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Camel market in Nouakchott
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Mauritania is almost 100% Muslim, with most inhabitants adhering to the <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> denomination.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> orders, the <a href="/wiki/Tijaniyah" class="mw-redirect" title="Tijaniyah">Tijaniyah</a> and the Qadiriyyah, have great influence not only in the country, but in Morocco, Algeria, Senegal and other neighborhood countries as well. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_Nouakchott" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of Nouakchott">Roman Catholic Diocese of Nouakchott</a>, founded in 1965, serves the 4,500 Catholics in Mauritania (mostly foreign residents from West Africa and Europe). </p>
      <p>There are extreme restrictions on freedom of religion and belief in Mauritania; it is one of thirteen countries in the world which punishes atheism by death.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> On 27 April 2018, The National Assembly passed a law that makes the death penalty mandatory for anyone convicted of "blasphemous speech" and acts deemed "sacrilegious". The new law eliminates the possibility under article 306 of substituting prison terms for the death penalty for certain apostasy-related crimes if the offender promptly repents. The law also provides for a sentence of up to two years in prison and a fine of up to 600,000 Ouguiyas (approximately EUR 14,630) for "offending public indecency and Islamic values" and for "breaching Allahâ€™s prohibitions" or assisting in their breach.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Mauritania" title="Languages of Mauritania">Languages of Mauritania</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> is the official and national language of Mauritania. The local spoken variety, known as <a href="/wiki/Hassaniya_Arabic" title="Hassaniya Arabic">Hassaniya</a>, contains many <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber</a> words and significantly differs from the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> that is used for official communication. <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Pulaar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soninke_language" title="Soninke language">Soninke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolof_language" title="Wolof language">Wolof</a> also serve as national languages.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> is widely used in the media and among educated classes.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Mauritania" title="Health in Mauritania">Health in Mauritania</a>
      </div> 
      <p>As of 2011, <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> at birth was 61.14 years.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> Per capita expenditure on health was 43 US$ (PPP) in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-61">[61]</a></sup> Public expenditure was 2% of the GDP in 2004 and private 0.9% of the GDP in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-61">[61]</a></sup> In the early 21st century, there were 11 physicians per 100,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-61">[61]</a></sup> Infant mortality is 60.42 deaths/1,000 live births (2011 estimate).<sup id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The obesity rate among Mauritanian women is high, perhaps in part due to the traditional standards of beauty (in some regions in the country), in which obese women are considered beautiful while thin women are considered sickly.<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc.msn.com_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc.msn.com-62">[62]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Mauritania" title="Education in Mauritania">Education in Mauritania</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Since 1999, all teaching in the first year of primary school is in <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> is introduced in the second year, and is used to teach all scientific courses.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> is increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Mauritania has the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nouakchott" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Nouakchott">University of Nouakchott</a> and other institutions of higher education, but the majority of highly educated Mauritanians have studied outside the country. Public expenditure on education was at 10.1% of 2000â€“2007 government expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-61">[61]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urbanization">Urbanization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Urbanization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mauritania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Mauritania" title="Regions of Mauritania">Regions of Mauritania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_Mauritania" title="Departments of Mauritania">Departments of Mauritania</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The government bureaucracy is composed of traditional ministries, special agencies, and <a href="/wiki/Parastatal" class="mw-redirect" title="Parastatal">parastatal</a> companies. The Ministry of Interior spearheads a system of regional governors and prefects modeled on the French system of local administration. Under this system, Mauritania is divided into 15 regions (<i><a href="/wiki/Wilaya" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilaya">wilaya</a></i> or <i>rÃ©gions</i>). </p>
      <p>Control is tightly concentrated in the executive branch of the central government, but a series of national and municipal elections since 1992 have produced limited <a href="/wiki/Decentralization" title="Decentralization">decentralization</a>. These regions are subdivided into 44 <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_Mauritania" title="Departments of Mauritania">departments</a> (<i>moughataa</i>). The regions and capital district (in alphabetical order) and their capitals are: </p>
